        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/cssc.201700628')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '90088877-ba5e-4fbd-b2b3-93e138cc1863'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

href="#references-section-1"><span>References</span></a></li><li><a class="w-slide__hide" href="#citedby-section"><span>Citing Literature</span></a></li></ul></div></div></div><div    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d25867964" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>What a waste</b>! The impact of different biowaste sources on the structural properties and electrochemical performance of hard carbons is still unclear. By unravelling and interlinking the properties of hard carbons with respect to the composition and structure of the biomass waste from which they are derived, this gap can be closed.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/439bd513-e00a-43e2-af8e-888f8d8d2564/cssc201700628-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/439bd513-e00a-43e2-af8e-888f8d8d2564/cssc201700628-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a7c7fd0f-dab0-42f7-a926-c548e787a24a/cssc201700628-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/439bd513-e00a-43e2-af8e-888f8d8d2564/cssc201700628-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d25867992" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbons are currently the most widely used negative electrode materials in Na-ion batteries. This is due to their promising electrochemical performance with capacities of 200–300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and stable long-term cycling. However, an abundant and cheap carbon source is necessary in order to comply with the low-cost philosophy of Na-ion technology. Many biological or waste materials have been used to synthesize hard carbons but the impact of the precursors on the final properties of the anode material is not fully understood. In this study the impact of the biomass source on the structural and electrochemical properties of hard carbons is unraveled by using different, representative types of biomass as examples. The systematic structural and electrochemical investigation of hard carbons derived from different sources—namely corncobs, peanut shells, and waste apples, which are representative of hemicellulose-, lignin- and pectin-rich biomass, respectively—enables understanding and interlinking of the structural and electrochemical properties.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc201700628-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered one of the most promising alternatives to lithium ion batteries (LIBs) for large scale energy storage applications due to their low cost and the abundance of the employed raw materials.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0001_R_d25867956e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Chem.">1</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0004_R_d25867956e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">4</a></span> Taking advantage of similarity with lithium-ion technology, sodium-ion technology has undergone rapidly increasing interest and consequent fast development in recent years at both research and development levels.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0005_R_d25867956e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Energy Environ. Sci.">5</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0007_R_d25867956e414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy Environ. Sci.">7</a></span> Nevertheless, there are several peculiar differences between the two technologies.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0008_R_d25867956e416" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Res.">8</a></span> One of the most striking is represented by the commonly used negative electrode material for LIBs, that is, graphite, which shows no comparable electrochemical performance in SIBs. Although it has been demonstrated that sodium can reversibly co-intercalate into graphite by using ether-based electrolytes, both in Na-metal<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0009_R_d25867956e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0011_R_d25867956e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a></span> and full Na-ion cell configuration,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0012_R_d25867956e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Power Sources">12</a></span> the corresponding capacity is relatively low, leading to limited energy densities.</p>
         
         <p>Hard carbons represent the most promising negative electrode materials for SIBs because of their high storage capacity and good cycling stability.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0013_R_d25867956e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Acc. Chem. Res.">13</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0014_R_d25867956e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">14</a></span> The first report on hard carbon as a suitable electrode material for SIBs dates back to 2000, when Stevens and Dahn reported the sodium uptake in glucose-derived hard carbon.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0015_R_d25867956e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">15</a></span> Since then, considerable progress <sup>[14]</sup> has been made to find a suitable hard carbon material that may play the role of graphite in LIBs. Nevertheless, there are a few issues to be addressed for the future commercialization and mass production of hard carbon. In fact, a low-cost and abundant carbon precursor is necessary for large-scale energy storage.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0016_R_d25867956e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0017_R_d25867956e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a></span> Also, hard carbon materials are characterized by large surface area, resulting in rather high first cycle irreversible capacities attributed to the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation, which consumes large amounts of sodium in full cells, requiring additional amounts of cathode material.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0013_R_d25867956e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Acc. Chem. Res.">13</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0018_R_d25867956e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a></span> The potential profile of hard carbons upon sodium uptake and release exhibits a sloping region (ca. 0.1–1.2 V) and a plateau at low voltages (≲0.1 V). The most commonly accepted sodium storage mechanism in hard carbons has long been considered to be the “house of cards” model.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0015_R_d25867956e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">15</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0019_R_d25867956e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a></span> According to this model, the first sloping potential (ca. 0.1–1.2 V) is attributed to the insertion of sodium ions between the graphene layers, whereas the low-potential plateau (≲0.1 V) is attributed to the insertion of sodium ions into the nanopores between the disordered graphene sheets. However, recent work has found the slope region to be mostly related to the storage of sodium at defect sites (e.g., heteroatoms like oxygen or nitrogen) rather than to reversible sodium (de-)intercalation.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0020_R_d25867956e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0021_R_d25867956e438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Lett.">21</a></span> Furthermore, converse results have been reported for the plateau at low voltage, linking the reversible sodium storage not only with a pore filling mechanism but also (de-)intercalation.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0021_R_d25867956e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Lett.">21</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0022_R_d25867956e440" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Biomass appears to be a very appropriate source of hard carbon. A study performed in 2003 revealed that around 730 Tg (1 Tg=10<sup>12</sup> g) of biomass is burned in a typical year, with about 34 % (i.e., ca. 250 Tg) contributed by crop residues burned in the fields.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0026_R_d25867956e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Global Biogeochem. Cycles">26</a></span> Open-air burning to dispose of biowaste generates CO<sub>2</sub> and other local pollutants, such as soot, volatile organic compounds (VOCs) and sulfur dioxide, which are responsible for the heavy smog in some Asian and South American countries.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0027_R_d25867956e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nature">27</a></span> Consequently, the need to recycling and reuse waste biomass has reached a consensus worldwide. Biofuels draw substantial attention in both academic and industrial circles.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0028_R_d25867956e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Science">28</a></span> However, the use of the cheapest and most abundant form of biomass, lignocellulose, is limited by the lack of low-cost processing technologies that can efficiently convert lignocellulosic biomass into liquid fuels.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0029_R_d25867956e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Rev.">29</a></span> Thus, technical processes involving the large-scale application of lignocellulosic materials are highly desirable and urgently needed. Additionally, biomass waste can be considered a reliable large-scale carbon source because the intensive “industrialization” of agriculture, due to the ever-increasing world population and improved living standards, leads to huge amounts of well-defined biomass and food waste.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0030_R_d25867956e457" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30">30</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Besides the low price and environmental friendliness, some biomass materials have specific microstructures that can be retained after carbonization. According to reports, hard carbon materials derived from different biomasses deliver specific capacities ranging from 100 to 350 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at low current densities.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0022_R_d25867956e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0031_R_d25867956e463" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Environ. Sci.">31</a></span> Compared with commercial precursors such as glucose, sucrose and polymers,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0014_R_d25867956e465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">14</a></span> biomass waste may represent an ideal carbon source. Indeed, several biomass wastes have been investigated, including banana peels,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0022_R_d25867956e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></span> pomelo peels,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0032_R_d25867956e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a></span> rice husks,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0033_R_d25867956e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Mater. Chem. A">33</a></span> ox horn,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0034_R_d25867956e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Mater. Chem. A">34</a></span> and peanut shells.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0035_R_d25867956e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochim. Acta">35</a></span> However, to our knowledge, no studies have been performed to evaluate the impact of different biowaste on the structural properties and electrochemical behavior of the derived hard carbons in order to establish a more rational material design.</p>
         
         <p>Of course, biomass waste is not a pure chemical. It is composed of several components, but is frequently free of cost if not subsidized (disposing of industrial biowaste disposal frequently carries additional costs). Generally, the major component of plant-derived biomass is cellulose. Cellulose molecules align to form microfibrils, which themselves are aligned and bound into macrofibrils by a matrix of hemicellulose, pectin, or lignin.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0036_R_d25867956e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. R. Soc. Interface">36</a></span> In consequence, cellulose-based biomass, in a simplified manner, can be categorized into the three subclasses belonging to pectin-, hemicellulose- and lignin-based materials. Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc201700628-tbl-0001">1</a> summarizes the different carbon sources according to the above-mentioned classification.
</p>
         
         <div class="article-table-content" id="cssc201700628-tbl-0001">
            <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table 1.
                    </span>Main binder components, as well as amounts of cellulose and main binder<span class="section-footNote"><span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0023_R_d25867956e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Bioresour. Technol.">23</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0025_R_d25867956e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Chem. Eng. J.">25</a></span></span> in the different dried carbon sources.</header>
            
            
            
            <div class="article-table-content-wrapper" tabindex="0">

                        <td class="left-aligned"><p>peanut shell</p></td>
                        
                        <td class="left-aligned"><p>Lignin</p></td>
                        
                        <td class="left-aligned"><p>27–40</p></td>
                        
                        <td class="left-aligned"><p>34–45</p></td>
                        </tr>
                     </tbody>
                  </table>
            </div>
            
            
            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>Herein we report a comparative study between hard carbons derived from waste materials belonging to the three different subclasses: peanut shells (lignin-based), corncobs (hemicellulose-based), and apples (pectin-based). The impact of these carbon sources on the physical characteristics and the electrochemical performances of the derived hard carbon materials [namely P-HC (pectin-based), H-HC (hemicellulose-based) and L-HC (lignin-based)] is evaluated. It is shown that the carbon source strongly affects the structural characteristics and the electrochemical behavior of the hard carbons.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc201700628-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201700628-sec-0003-title"> Synthesis and characterization of biomass-derived hard carbons</h3>
            
            <p>The synthetic route for corncob and peanut-shell biomass-derived hard carbons is schematically depicted in Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0001">1</a>, whereas that for waste apple has been reported previously.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0037_R_d25867956e607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></span> It should be noted, however, that the synthesis of P-HC from waste apples involved phosphoric acid treatment for only three days, since longer treatments resulted in very low yields (≤0.5 % for two weeks’ treatment).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc201700628-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d6843472-ad63-42cf-8193-67e5e1cd426f/cssc201700628-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d6843472-ad63-42cf-8193-67e5e1cd426f/cssc201700628-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9b73ee05-f759-41ad-82ca-d71d1197e37d/cssc201700628-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d6843472-ad63-42cf-8193-67e5e1cd426f/cssc201700628-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201700628-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201700628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Synthetic pathway for the different biowaste-derived hard carbons.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Phosphoric acid is used as an activating agent, playing a key role in the process, as described later.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0032_R_d25867956e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Mater. Chem. A">32</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0038_R_d25867956e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38">38</a></span> Suspensions containing apple biowaste and corncob turned black and jelly-like after one day, whereas the suspension of peanut shells remained transparent and liquid-like. This difference is explained by considering that apples contain pectin, alongside glucose and fructose, and corncob contains hemicellulose. Both of these chemicals contain α-connected units (i.e., connecting oxygen in axial position), which are known to be prone to hydrolysis in aqueous solutions.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0039_R_d25867956e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Molecules">39</a></span> Consequently, treatment in concentrated phosphoric acid leads to the destruction of the polysaccharides and, hence, of the fibril structure of the biomass, which explains the formation of jelly-like suspensions. In contrast, lignin from peanut shells is more stable against hydrolysis or protonation in acids and no black colloids are formed upon acid treatment of peanut shells, resulting in retention of a large fraction of the compact fibril structure.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0039_R_d25867956e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Molecules">39</a></span> The water-rinsing step is performed to remove the black colloids and other water-soluble impurities. The subsequent thermal treatment leads to the carbonization of the acid treated biomass, involving the release of H<sub>2</sub>O, CO<sub>2</sub>, and other gaseous species. As a consequence, the carbonization step leads to different yields of 38.2 %, 24.7 %, and 33.2 % for L-HC, H-HC and H-HC, respectively, reflecting the stability of the biomass in acid and its heteroatom content. The yields were calculated with respect to the dried acid-treated and water-rinsed biomass.</p>
            
            <p>The structural properties of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> and H-HC have been investigated by XRD (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0002">2</a> a, b) and Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0002">2</a> c, d), whereas the morphology was investigated by SEM analysis (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0002">2</a> e, f).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc201700628-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e534c342-3eaf-4bf8-b815-88889b080c09/cssc201700628-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e534c342-3eaf-4bf8-b815-88889b080c09/cssc201700628-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/861dda0c-a0ba-4cc1-945c-1b7085ff5f09/cssc201700628-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e534c342-3eaf-4bf8-b815-88889b080c09/cssc201700628-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201700628-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201700628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Structural and morphological characterization of L-HC and H-HC hard carbons, including X-ray diffraction patterns (a, b), Raman spectra (c, d), and SEM images (e, f).</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figures <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0002">2</a> a and b show the characteristic (002) and (101) reflections of hard carbons at 2<i>θ</i>=22° and 43°, respectively. The calculated average interlayer spacings (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) of L-HC and H-HC, obtained from the (002) reflection via the Bragg equation, are rather similar (about 0.408 nm and 0.406 nm, respectively). The additional reflection at 2<i>θ</i>=26.5° in the diffraction pattern of the L-HC material is attributed to the (002) reflection of graphitic carbon. Interestingly, this is not observed in H-HC.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0040_R_d25867956e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Science">40</a></span> This may be due to the higher acid stability of the lignin-based precursor, leading to the large extent of the macrofibrillar structure being retained. The presence of graphitic carbon might be beneficial for the electrochemical performance due to the enhanced electronic conductivity. The Raman spectra (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0002">2</a> c, d) of L-HC and H-HC exhibit a broad <span class="smallCaps">D</span>-band (around 1340 cm<sup>−1</sup>), ascribed to a defect-induced mode, and G-bands (around 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>), ascribed to the E<sub>2g</sub> graphitic mode. The intensity ratio of these bands (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) is commonly used as an index of the carbon materials’ disorder.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0041_R_d25867956e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></span> The intensity ratio of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> is 0.98, which is lower than that of H-HC (1.01), indicating a more ordered structure of the former material.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0042_R_d25867956e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">42</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0043_R_d25867956e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nanoscale">43</a></span> SEM images reveal that <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0002">2</a> e) retains a fibril-like structure, whereas H-HC (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0002">2</a> f) is composed of inhomogeneous particles.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0037_R_d25867956e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></span> In summary, the morphological and the structural results presented above confirm the highest stability of the lignin-based biomass against hydrolysis.</p>
            
            <p>Characteristics of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span>, H-HC and P-HC are summarized in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc201700628-tbl-0002">2</a>. P-HC shows a lower calculated average interlayer spacing (about 0.385 nm) and higher structural disorder (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>=1.26) than <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> and H-HC. Nitrogen adsorption–desorption measurements (BET) reveal the surface areas of H-HC and P-HC to be rather similar and substantially higher than that of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span>, reflecting the higher acid stability of lignin-based biomass. The comparison of the elemental composition of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> and H-HC indicates higher nitrogen and carbon contents in the former material, but a higher residual content, typically considered a good indicator of the oxygen content, in H-HC, whereas P-HC contains more hydrogen and carbon and less residual elements than L-HC and H-HC. This can be explained by the shorter storage time in phosphoric acid (i.e., three days vs. two weeks).
</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="cssc201700628-tbl-0002">
               <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table 2.

               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The porosity of the as-prepared hard carbons was investigated by nitrogen adsorption–desorption measurements (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0003">3</a> a). The DFT pore-size distribution (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0003">3</a> b) shows the existence of small mesopores with an average pore width of about 2.7 nm in all hard carbons. However, the lowest fraction is observed for P-HC, whereas H-HC and <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> have similar pore volumes. Interestingly, a significant fraction of slightly larger mesopores (3.7 nm) is detected for L-HC. In contrast, P-HC and H-HC show, respectively, a low fraction and nearly no mesopores of this width. A recent work by Tarascon and co-workers demonstrated mesoporosity, rather than the microporosity, to be crucial for the reversible Na-ion storage in hard carbons along the plateau (0.02–0.1 V).<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0020_R_d25867956e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></span> Based on these findings, different electrochemical behaviors can be anticipated for the different biowaste-derived hard carbons.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc201700628-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/311b186b-6c24-4613-8814-013d9c20905e/cssc201700628-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/311b186b-6c24-4613-8814-013d9c20905e/cssc201700628-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ca9291fe-2aff-4b47-80f4-62c26c08b0f8/cssc201700628-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/311b186b-6c24-4613-8814-013d9c20905e/cssc201700628-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201700628-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201700628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms (a) and DFT pore size distributions (b) for L-HC, H-HC, and P-HC.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To further understand the impact of the various biomasses on the resulting hard carbons, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analyses of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span>, H-HC and P-HC were performed in the carbon and oxygen regions (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0004">4</a> and Figure S1 in the Supporting Information).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc201700628-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7f69bc99-7f55-499e-8c6d-fb2b9ed66a21/cssc201700628-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7f69bc99-7f55-499e-8c6d-fb2b9ed66a21/cssc201700628-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8331ae8c-51ee-4397-84f9-bbab10708f02/cssc201700628-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7f69bc99-7f55-499e-8c6d-fb2b9ed66a21/cssc201700628-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201700628-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201700628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>X-ray photoelectron spectra of L-HC, H-HC, and P-HC. a–c) C 1s core-level spectra; d–f) O 1s core-level spectra.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0004">4</a> a–c and d–f show the C 1s and O 1s core-level spectra, respectively. In the C 1s region, four peaks are observed at binding energies of 284.6, 285.1, 286.5, and 290.0 eV, which are associated to C=C, C−H, C−O, and C=O groups, respectively. In the O 1s region, three peaks are seen at binding energies of 530.8, 532.3, and 533.2 eV, associated to O−H-, C=O-, and C−O-containing species, respectively.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0044_R_d25867956e981" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0046_R_d25867956e981" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">46</a></span></p>
            
            <p>The percentage share of the various carbons is summarized in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc201700628-tbl-0002">2</a>. However, it should be noted that the binding energy of the C−C (sp<sup>3</sup>) bond falls between that of C=C (sp<sup>2</sup>) and that of C−H (sp<sup>3</sup>), which makes the fitting of the C 1s core level spectra impossible. The quantitative determination of the C−C (sp<sup>3</sup>) carbon species was therefore not performed. Instead, their contribution was considered together with either C−H (sp<sup>3</sup>) (C−H+C−C) or C=C (sp<sup>2</sup>) (C=C+C−C) species (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc201700628-tbl-0002">2</a>).</p>
            
            <p>In general, the XPS elemental analysis (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc201700628-tbl-0002">2</a>) is in good accordance with the results obtained from CHN elemental analysis. The slight differences can be attributed to the fact that XPS is limited to the surface region, whereas the elemental analysis probes the bulk composition. The difference in the amount of carbon detected by the two techniques is negligible for L-HC and P-HC. However, for H-HC rather different values are obtained, in accordance with the higher amount of impurities, as indicated by the high residual content in CHN analysis of this material. The lower nitrogen and higher residual contents found by CHN analysis can be explained by the higher content of adsorbed impurities,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0047_R_d25867956e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">47</a></span> and is in good accordance with the higher surface area of H-HC compared to L-HC. The impurities, of course, affect the electrochemical properties, such as the irreversible capacity, as discussed below.</p>
            
            <p>Therefore, L-HC and H-HC contain similar amounts of sp<sup>2</sup>- and sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized carbon, but H-HC contains more impurities and P-HC more sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized carbon. These results are in good agreement with the data obtained by XPS (Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc201700628-tbl-0003">3</a>). In summary, it can be stated that the nature of the biomass (and the main binder component therein) has a strong impact on the composition and structural characteristics of the resulting hard carbons.
</p>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="cssc201700628-tbl-0003">
               <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table 3.
                    </span>Atomic percentage of carbon and oxygen, as well as the relative amounts (in percent) of C=C/C−C-, C−H/C−C-, C−O-, and C=O-containing species, as obtained by XPS analysis of the C 1s and O 1s core-level spectra.</header>

                           </tr>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201700628-sec-0004-title"> Electrochemical characterization of L-HC and H-HC</h3>
            
            <p>To understand the impact of composition and structural characteristics on the electrochemical performance, L-HC and H-HC were investigated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) performed at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> within the 0.02–3.00 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) potential range (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0005">5</a> a,b).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc201700628-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fd1fd723-abdb-48da-b7eb-a17f546af680/cssc201700628-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/fd1fd723-abdb-48da-b7eb-a17f546af680/cssc201700628-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a442d0e9-21a5-4acd-91d4-f7b35e967029/cssc201700628-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fd1fd723-abdb-48da-b7eb-a17f546af680/cssc201700628-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201700628-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201700628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performances of L-HC and H-HC: a, b) Cyclic voltammograms (1st, 5th, and 10th cycle) of L-HC and H-HC at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> in a potential range of 0.02–3.0 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). c, d) Galvanostatic long-term cycling at 0.1C.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Generally (i.e., after the 1st cycle), the peak couple below 0.3 V in the cathodic and anodic scans is attributed to the charge accumulation occurring in the pores, although, as mentioned in the Introduction, this interpretation of the voltammetric features is still debated.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0048_R_d25867956e1308" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Energy">48</a></span> The current observed at 0.2–1.5 V is associated to the storage of sodium at defect sites and between disordered graphene sheets.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0019_R_d25867956e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0021_R_d25867956e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Lett.">21</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0049_R_d25867956e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Mater.">49</a></span></p>
            
            <p>The cathodic scans of the first cyclic sweep reveal that almost no electrochemical reaction occurs from open circuit voltage (OCV) until 1.0 V. The current increase between 1.0 V and 0.1 V is associated to both the sodium storage mechanism and the SEI formation.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0019_R_d25867956e1314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0021_R_d25867956e1314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano Lett.">21</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0049_R_d25867956e1314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Mater.">49</a></span> In contrast, the current peaks at 0.3 V and 0.6 V for L-HC and at 0.35 V for H-HC were not detected in the first cathodic scan of P-HC in our previous study.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0037_R_d25867956e1316" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></span> They are probably related to the sodiation of impurities, which are present in <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> and H-HC to a higher extent than in P-HC, as evidenced by elemental analysis. During the first anodic scan, a broad current peak is detected for both L-HC and H-HC between 0.02 V and 0.20 V, which increases upon consecutive sweeps, suggesting progressive structural changes of the hard carbon upon sodium uptake and release.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0022_R_d25867956e1320" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Nano">22</a></span></p>
            
            <p>To investigate the electrochemical behavior of the developed materials, galvanostatic cycling tests were performed in sodium metal cells. Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0005">5</a> c–f shows the electrochemical cycling performance of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> and H-HC. In the first cycle, a rather low current rate (0.02C) was used to activate the electrodes (SEI formation). Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0005">5</a> c and d depict the electrochemical behavior of L-HC and H-HC, respectively, upon long-term cycling at 0.1C. During the 1st cycle, the coulombic efficiency of L-HC and H-HC are 68 % and 57 %, respectively, whereas the initial coulombic efficiency for P-HC in our previous study was 61 %.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0037_R_d25867956e1331" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></span> The low first cycle efficiency is usually related to electrolyte decomposition, leading to SEI formation, and irreversible sodium uptake by heteroatoms such as oxygen. The coulombic efficiency results are, overall, in good agreement with the differences in BET surface area and elemental composition of the hard carbon materials. Indeed, L-HC exhibits the highest 1st cycle coulombic efficiency and the lowest BET surface area. P-HC and H-HC have similar surface areas, but H-HC contains more heteroatoms than P-HC, giving the lowest 1st cycle coulombic efficiency. Nevertheless, both L-HC and H-HC show good coulombic efficiencies, up to 99 %, already after 5 cycles. In terms of sodium storage performance (at 0.1 C) a neat difference is observed between L-HC, which delivers a capacity of 298 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and H-HC, exhibiting a capacity of only 257 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, L-HC showed an excellent capacity retention (98 %) after 300 cycles.</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0005">5</a> e and f compares the rate capabilities of L-HC and H-HC. <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> delivers capacities of around 299, 287, 266, 236, 175, 104, and 77 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and H-HC capacities of 254, 242, 218.6, 191, 151, 103, and 83 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, at 0.1C, 0.2C, 0.5C, 1C, 2C, 5 C and 10 C, respectively. When the current rate is decreased back to 0.1C (i.e., after the rate test), the specific capacities of <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> and H-HC reverted to 297 and 254 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The almost complete capacity retention demonstrates the good stability of the materials towards high rates. Figure S2 a and b shows selected potential profiles of L-HC and H-HC measured during the C-rate test at different current rates (from 0.1 C to 10 C). Considering the previous results reported for P-HC,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0037_R_d25867956e1354" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></span> it is worth noting that all hard carbons deliver very similar capacities at high current rate such as 5 C and 10 C. Their potential profiles show that the plateau at about 0.1 V shifts below the lower cutoff potential at high currents, resulting in the slope region to contribute to the delivered capacity.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0037_R_d25867956e1356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></span></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201700628-sec-0005-title"> Impact of biomass source on the electrochemical properties</h3>
            
            <p>To further link the electrochemical performance of the different hard carbons to their structural characteristics, the charge/discharge voltage profiles recorded in the 5th cycle of L-HC, H-HC, and P-HC are compared (Figure <a href="#cssc201700628-fig-0006">6</a>). All hard carbon electrodes show the typical sodium storage behavior with a sloping potential profile at 1.0–0.12 V and a subsequent plateau below 0.12 V. The sloped part of all hard carbons is practically overlapping, but the low-voltage plateau (i.e., below 0.12 V) of L-HC is more extended than those of H-HC and, especially, P-HC. The specific capacities at 1.0–0.12 V (slope) and 0.12–0.02 V (plateau) during the 5th discharge are shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#cssc201700628-tbl-0004">4</a>. The electrochemical behavior of the different hard carbons can be understood by comparing their capacities and connecting these with the measured structural properties. The slope region (1.0 V–0.12 V) is most frequently explained by the reaction of sodium with O and N heteroatoms, as well as further sodium uptake through insertion into and adsorption on graphene-like sheets.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0019_R_d25867956e1370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0020_R_d25867956e1370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></span> The capacity delivered/consumed within this voltage range (1.0–0.12 V) only slightly decreases upon going from <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> to P-HC. <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span> shows the highest capacity in this voltage range, most likely because it has a higher interlayer spacing (XRD), a higher amount of O and N heteroatoms (CHN, XPS), and a better electronic conductivity, due to the presence of graphite (XRD), and finally a higher structural order (Raman <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio). H-HC shows a slightly lower capacity, which fits well to the slightly lower structural order (Raman) and the absence of graphitic carbon (XRD), but similar average interlayer spacing and content of heteroatoms on the surface (XPS), and finally a higher content of impurities (high residual content in CHN). Finally, P-HC has the lowest capacity, owing to the smallest average interlayer spacing (XRD), lowest content of heteroatoms O and N (CHN, XPS), and, additionally, the most disordered structure (i.e., high amount of sp<sup>3</sup>-hybridized carbon and no graphitic carbon; Raman, XPS).
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="cssc201700628-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e186b6c0-a8e1-4414-9a88-9a0de0a5a289/cssc201700628-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e186b6c0-a8e1-4414-9a88-9a0de0a5a289/cssc201700628-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/645a7e3d-680d-4234-9f52-e3487a8a01de/cssc201700628-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e186b6c0-a8e1-4414-9a88-9a0de0a5a289/cssc201700628-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=cssc201700628-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fcssc.201700628" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Comparison of the electrochemical performances of L-HC, H-HC, and P-HC with charge/discharge profiles at 0.1 C in the 5th cycle.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="article-table-content" id="cssc201700628-tbl-0004">
               <header class="article-table-caption"><span class="table-caption__label">Table 4.

               <div class="article-table-content-wrapper" tabindex="0">
                  <table class="table article-section__table">
                     
                     
                     
                     
                     <thead>

                           <th class="bottom-bordered-cell left-aligned"><p>Capacity (0.12 V–0.02 V)</p></th>
                           </tr>
                        </thead>
                     <tbody>
                        
                        <tr>
                           
                           <td class="right-bordered-cell left-aligned"><p><span class="smallCaps">L</span>-HC</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>131.79 mAh g<sup>−1</sup></p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>158.81 mAh g<sup>−1</sup></p></td>
                           </tr>
                        
                        <tr>
                           
                           <td class="right-bordered-cell left-aligned"><p>H-HC</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>119.58 mAh g<sup>−1</sup></p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>144.11 mAh g<sup>−1</sup></p></td>
                           </tr>
                        
                        <tr>
                           
                           <td class="right-bordered-cell left-aligned"><p>P-HC</p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>111.76 mAh g<sup>−1</sup></p></td>
                           
                           <td class="left-aligned"><p>108.24 mAh g<sup>−1</sup></p></td>
                           </tr>
                        </tbody>
                     </table>
               </div>
               
               
               <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The plateau region (0.12 V–0.02 V) is commonly attributed to charge accumulation in the pores.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0019_R_d25867956e1493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">19</a>, <a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0050_R_d25867956e1493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Chem. Mater.">50</a></span> Recent work demonstrated that the extent of this region strongly depends on the hard carbon's mesoporosity. The formation of small mesopores occurs significantly at carbonization temperatures above 950 °C.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0016_R_d25867956e1495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></span> The amount of mesopores is consistent with that of graphitic carbon upon increasing carbonization temperature. At carbonization temperatures of 2800 °C, mostly mesopores are present in hard carbons.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0020_R_d25867956e1497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></span> Therefore, a higher content of graphitic carbon (i.e., sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon) indicates a higher amount of available mesopores and, hence, an extended low-voltage plateau upon (de-)sodiation.<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0020_R_d25867956e1501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></span> This is the case for <span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span>, which shows higher structural order (Raman), a higher content of sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon (XPS), and the presence of graphite (XRD). Indeed, nitrogen adsorption–desorption measurements revealed the highest content of small mesopores (i.e., those between 2.7 nm and 3.7 nm in diameter) in L-HC. H-HC has a similar structural order (amount of sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon), but does not contain graphitic carbon, has a higher amount of impurities and a much lower fraction of small mesopores of 3.7 nm, all resulting in the lower capacity delivered/consumed by such a material in the low potential range. Finally, P-HC contains the largest amount of sp<sup>3</sup>- hybridized carbon, with the largest structural disorder and the lowest concentration of mesopores, which agrees well with the low capacity in this range.</p>
            
            <p>In summary, our results show that a stable and reproducible electrochemical performance can be obtained for biowaste-derived hard carbons. Moreover, our investigation strongly indicates that the mesoporosity of hard carbons determines the extent of the potential plateau below 0.12 V, whereas the sloping part of the potential profile (1.0–0.12 V) mainly depends on the amount of heteroatoms and interlayer spacing. These parameters all depend on the composition and structure of the biomass source, as shown for typical representatives of hemicellulose-, lignin- and pectin-rich biomass.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0006">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc201700628-sec-0006-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Different waste biomass sources, distinguished with respect to their main binder component (i.e., lignin, hemicellulose or pectin) have been investigated as precursors for the synthesis of hard carbons. In particular, peanut shells and corncobs, respectively containing lignin and hemicellulose, were successfully transformed into hard carbons. Their properties and electrochemical performances were compared with those of apple (pectin)-derived hard carbon.</p>
         
         <p>Acid treatment was identified as an important step in the synthesis, as the various binder components are differently sensitive towards hydrolysis, which strongly affects the characteristics of the derived hard carbons. The composition and structure of the various biomasses crucially influences the process yield, surface area, elemental composition (bulk and surface), structural disorder and impurity content of the resulting hard carbons.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, the electrochemical behavior of the various hard carbons, in terms of reversible and irreversible capacities, shape of the potential profile upon sodiation and desodiation, and cyclability, could be clearly linked to characteristic properties of the waste biomass precursors. Among the investigated materials, that derived from peanut shells (<span class="smallCaps">L-HC</span>) showed the best specific capacity (298 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), the highest capacity retention (98 % after 300 cycles at a low current rate of 0.1 C) and the highest 1<sup>st</sup> cycle coulombic efficiency (68 %) among all the materials. The results reported herein demonstrate that biowaste not only represents an appealing renewable resource for carbonaceous materials, but may also yield high-performance anodes for sodium-ion batteries. In particular, the good electrochemical performance of hard carbons derived from peanut shells needs to be highlighted as proof that lignocellulosic biomass waste cannot only be used for the generation of biofuels.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0007">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc201700628-sec-0007-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0008">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201700628-sec-0008-title"> Synthesis of biowaste-derived hard carbon</h3>
            
            <p>Peanut shells and corncobs were cut into pieces and dried at 80 °C for 72 hours. The resulting materials were manually ground into powders, then completely wetted with phosphoric acid (80 wt.%) and stored for 2 weeks under ambient atmosphere in a fume hood [for P-HC, apple biowastes were wet with phosphoric acid (80 wt %) for 3 days<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0037_R_d25867956e1549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ChemElectroChem">37</a></span>]. Afterwards, deionized water was added and the suspension was filtered. Subsequently, the solid fraction was rinsed with deionized water until the pH was close to 7.0, then dried at 80 °C overnight, transferred into a quartz tube, annealed at 1100 °C under argon atmosphere for 1 h (heating rate 1 °C min<sup>−1</sup>) and allowed to cool in the furnace.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0009">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201700628-sec-0009-title"> Material characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The structure and morphology of hard carbon materials were investigated by means of X-ray diffraction (XRD, Bruker D8 Advance diffractometer with Cu<sub>Kα0</sub> radiation) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Zeiss Auriga<sup>®</sup>). The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area and pore width was determined by nitrogen adsorption measurements using an ASAP (Accelerated Surface Area and Porosimetry) 2020 analyzer (Micrometrics). CHN elemental analysis was performed with an Elementar vario MICRO cube. Raman measurements were performed with a confocal InVia Raman micro spectrometer with a 633 nm laser (Renishaw; each spectrum was taken as the average of three 10-second accumulations). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) characterization was performed with a PHI 5800 MultiTechnique ESCA System, using monochromatized AlKα (1486.6 eV) radiation. The measurements were performed with a detection angle of 45°, using pass energies at the analyzer of 187.85 and 29.35 eV for survey and detail spectra, respectively. All XP spectra were calibrated to the signal of amorphous carbon (hard carbon) at 284.8 eV. Peak deconvolution was done using CasaXPS software. After binding energy calibration, a standard Shirley background was implemented. In the next step, a set of peaks accounting for the expected species was introduced. The peak positions were set using previously reported data,<span><a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0044_R_d25867956e1566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">44</a>-<a href="#cssc201700628-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="cssc201700628-bib-0046_R_d25867956e1566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">46</a></span> and kept at the same value for all samples. For the peak shape, the commonly applied mixture of Gaussian and Lorentzian line shape (G/L 70:30) was used.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0010">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201700628-sec-0010-title"> Electrode preparation and cell assembly</h3>
            
            <p>Electrodes were prepared with a dry composition of hard carbon (80 wt %), conductive carbon (10 wt %; SuperC45, Imerys), and sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC, 10 wt %; WALOCEL CRT 2000 PPA 12, Dow Wolff Cellulosics). CMC was first dissolved in deionized water to give a 5 wt % solution. The conductive additive and hard carbon were added to the CMC solution and the resulting mixture was dispersed by ball milling for 2.5 h (70 min with 10 min rest; 1 repetition; main disk speed: 400 rpm; rotating planets speed: −800 rpm). The obtained slurry (solid content: 14.3 %; wet thickness: 150 μm) was casted with a doctor blade on a dendritic copper foil at room temperature. Afterwards the coated electrodes were dried at 80 °C for 10 minutes and then at room temperature overnight under ambient atmosphere. Disk electrodes (Ø=12 mm) were punched and dried for 2 h at 20 °C and 10 h at 150 °C under vacuum in a glass oven. The average active material mass loading of the electrodes was around 1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="cssc201700628-sec-0011">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="cssc201700628-sec-0011-title"> Electrochemical characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Three-electrode Swagelok cells were assembled using the hard carbons as the working electrode (WE) and sodium metal (99.8 %, Acros Organics) as the counter (CE) and reference (RE) electrodes. All potential values given in this manuscript refer to the Na/Na<sup>+</sup> quasi-reference electrode. Whatman glass fiber GF/D disks were used as separators and soaked with 240 μL of electrolyte (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/propylene carbonate (1:1 w/w). Sodium perchlorate (98 % Sigma Aldrich) propylene carbonate (Sigma Aldrich), and ethylene carbonate (UBE) were used as received. Electrolyte preparation and cell assembly were carried out in a glove box (MBraun) with oxygen and water contents below 0.1 ppm.</p>
            
            <p>Galvanostatic cycling tests, between 0.02 V and 2.0 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), were carried out with a battery tester (Maccor series 4000, U.S.A). The specific current of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> is defined as 1C. Cyclic voltammetry was performed between 0.02 V and 3.0 V (vs. Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) by using a multichannel potentiostat–galvanostat (VMP3, Biologic Science Instruments). All electrochemical tests were performed in climatic chambers at 20±1 °C.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="cssc201700628-sec-0012-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>

         <ol></ol>

                  <li data-bib-id="cssc201700628-bib-0005"><span class="bullet">5</span><span class="author">J. Peters</span>, <span class="author">D. Buchholz</span>, <span class="author">S.
